        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>Pedroia, not unexpectedly, fell off slightly from his 2008 MVP season, as almost all of his offensive numbers were down. The main reason for the decline was that his BABIP fell from .336 to .300, which took his batting average and slugging with it. Still, 2009 was a good year, as he posted a wOBA of .360, third best amongst second basemen. In addition, his walk rate (10.6%) and strikeout rate (7.2%) actually improved, and he is a rare player who walks more than he strikes out. Another promising trend is that in 2009 he hit more fly balls than in 2008 but fewer infield fly balls. Many of those fly balls were doubles, thanks to Fenway, but if he adds power as he ages, they could become home runs. <br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>Pedroia is one of the top second basemen going into the 2010 season. His solid contact skills mean he should have an average around .300. He bats first or second for a very good offense and has great on-base skills, so he should score around 100 runs. Since he is still young, Pedroia has a solid shot at adding power and having a 20/20 (HR/SB) season, but even a conservative estimate would give him around 15 of each. He should have around 75 RBIs batting second in a high-OBP offense. If your league counts doubles, Pedroia’s value rises, as he should be among the league leaders. Providing decent home run and RBI numbers with a very good batting average and run totals at a scarce position makes him an elite fantasy player. (Dave Allen)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>One of many Red Sox players to hit the disabled list for an extended period of time in 2010, Pedroia will return in 2011 from a broken foot with no reason to be skeptical of his performance going forward. His average has dipped a bit over the last few seasons, from .326 to .296 to .288 (BABIPs: .331, .297, .291, respectively), but he's made up for it with increased power (career-best .205 ISO in 2010, by far). Pedroia will be productive as long as he stays healthy, and there's no reason not to expect him to be. The foot injury was a fluke, suffered on a foul tip. He'll give owners a high batting average with 15 or more homers and one of the highest runs-scored totals in the game, and he's also good for 20 steals. With no worse than average production in four of the five traditional scoring categories, Pedroia should be the third second baseman off the board behind Robinson Cano and Chase Utley. It would not be stunning if he outproduced those two in 2011 either. (Mike Axisa)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>The only thing that stopped Pedroia from another brilliant season in 2010 was a broken foot, and there's no reason not to expect a rebound in 2011. Solid or better production in all five traditional scoring categories from a middle infielder? Yes, please. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Pedroia came back strong, posting the first 20/20 campaign of his career while pushing his average back over .300. You can count on him to reliably be in the top five in every standard category at second base. Last season may end up his peak in terms of WAR thanks to his outsized defensive performance, but his offense should stay at the same level. In his first five full seasons, Pedroia has not posted a wOBA lower than .360, which is a claim that Robinson Cano, Ian Kinsler and Dan Uggla cannot make. As an added bonus, he mashed lefties even more than usual last season -- his .435 wOBA against lefties was the seventh-best in the majors among qualified hitters. Grab him early. (Paul Swydan)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Dustin Pedroia continues to be one of the best second basemen in the game, and while second base is a deep position, you shouldn’t hesitate to grab him very early in your draft.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>It was definitely a tale of two seasons for Pedroia. In the first half, he had to deal with both injury (to his thumbs) and distraction (Bobby Valentine vs. Kevin Youkilis), and it showed in his performance -- he hit just .266/.326/.400. The malaise did not continue in the second half though, as he hit a much more Pedroia-like .318/.372/.508, and flashed both the power and speed that we are accustomed to seeing from the diminutive California native. The second half numbers couldn't save him from the worst season of his career -- his .344 wOBA was 15 points lower than any of his other full five seasons -- but it is an encouraging sign moving as we look to 2013. How much his first half dampens his value is a matter of debate. On the one hand, his full-season numbers don't look as strong, particularly in the RBI department. On the other, even with the diminished numbers, he's still pretty clearly a top-five player at the keystone, and may still go high as a result. But whether he slides in your draft or not, he should still be a player in which you feel confident. And there are few second baseman that you can say that about. (Paul Swydan)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Even after the worst season of his big league career, Pedroia remains a top-five second baseman, particularly after his strong second half.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Boston's pint-sized second baseman continued to put up top-five numbers at second base in 2013, although the distribution of his value was a bit different than year's past. An uptick in both batting average on balls in play and walk rate helped push both his batting average and on-base percentage up after a somewhat suppressed (and Bobby Valentine-filled) 2012. Unfortunately for owners needing help in the homers category, Pedroia's power has slowly dried up over the last three years. After hitting a career-high 21 jacks in 2011, he has only posted marks of 15 and nine over the last two seasons. The home run fall has correlated well with a drop in home runs per fly ball as well as a small decline in batted ball distance. However, it's tough to assume Pedroia's power has completely evaporated in his late-20's. He also tore the UCL in his thumb early in the season and played through the injury all season, only opting for surgery after Boston's Duck Boat parade. Perhaps 20 homers is a stretch without some  luck, but it wouldn't be outlandish to think he could get back into the low teens. With his batting eye and run/RBI totals hitting at the top of the Boston lineup, he's a second-round pick again in 2014. (<a href="http://www.twitter.com/soxczar">Colin Zarzycki</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Pedroia traded some power for batting average in 2013, but maintained top-five value among second basemen. With a torn UCL in his thumb surgically repaired, look for Pedroia to continue to maintain his status as one of the league's elite middle infielders in 2014.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Another year, another drop in the power department for Boston's diminutive second baseman. The 31-year-old only hit seven home runs in over 600 plate appearances in 2014. He hadn't been under that number since his 2006 cup of coffee (two homers in 98 plate appearances). Part of the reason for this slide may have been... a literal slide. Carlos Gomez plowed into Pedroia during the first week of last year's campaign, causing the infielder to land on his left wrist. This injury apparently plagued him throughout the campaign and he eventually shut it down in September to have doctors perform a "first dorsal compartment release and a tenosynovectomy." That does not sound pleasant. Even with the injury, Pedroia posted above-average (for him, at least) line drive rates and fly ball distance, and his desire to play through the injury still gave him top-12 value at second base by the end of the season (quantity over quality, baby!). He'll be an incredibly tough guy to rank in 2015, with his ranking hinging on the health and strength of his arms. Let him go if someone wants to draft him among the top six or so second basemen, there's too much risk there. If he falls towards back-end starting second base or middle infield material, however, don't be afraid to roll the dice on finding positive value in the middle-late rounds. (Colin Zarzycki)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Dustin Pedroia hurt his arm/wrist/finger/whatever again. Dustin Pedroia's power output continued to crash down to earth. Dustin Pedroia had surgery to fix said ailment. Don't draft Dustin Pedroia like you would have two years ago, but if he's healthy, maybe Dustin Pedroia isn't such a terrible choice if you still need a middle infielder in the middle rounds.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>The bummer about Pedroia missing 69 games (not nice) last year is that he was still pretty good when he did play. He had an .819 OPS in 69 nice games before first hitting the disabled list in late June. He tried to come back after essentially a minimum stay but went just 1-for-23 before heading right back for the same injury -- a strained right hamstring -- for another month-plus. However, he did finish his season on the field and closed with a bang, posting a .308/.386/.500 in 88 plate appearances. While the hamstring tanked the season from a volume standpoint, his base skills held firm and his isolated slugging percentage was actually a three-year high at .150. At 32, Pedroia is in that phase of career that Chase Utley just finished where we must plan for time missed, but the time you do get will still yield strong stats. Pedroia probably won't continue to run as Utley did -- you could say Pedroia has already stopped as he's been just 8-for-16 the last two years -- but his average should remain high, so it works out as a trade-off. Additionally, there is a measure of depth at second base that makes securing a backup much easier. If you want high floor at the position, pair him with someone like DJ LeMahieu; if upside is your play, gamble on Devon Travis as your second option at the position -- even if the Blue Jay is likely to miss upwards of a month to start the season. (<a href="http://thespguide.com/">Paul Sporer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>If you only play 93 games, your season was a disappointment on the fantasy landscape. Except if you're Giancarlo Stanton or Troy Tulowitzki, as they're usually able to put up almost a full season of work in under 100 games. If you build in some missed time for Pedroia and plan accordingly, he can still be a solid fantasy asset -- especially in that offense.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Following his struggles in 2014 and in 2015, some with health, some with the bat, it was fair to wonder whether Dustin Pedroia would ever be an elite hitter again. He still had defense as his calling card - even in 2014, when he slipped to a career-worst 98 wRC+, just below the league-adjusted average offensive productivity, he was still worth 4.1 WAR on the strength of his defense. But in both 2013 and '14, he failed to crack double digits in home runs, and in 2014 his doubles were down, and he failed to record a triple for the first time in any full major league season. His power evaporated, and his walk rate slipped - the .337 on-base percentage he posted in 2014 was easily a full-season worst. He turned things around somewhat in 2015, but he got hurt and only logged 93 games, an unfathomable total for the gritty-to-the-point-of-reckless Pedroia. But in 2016, everything clicked back into place. The 120 wRC+ and .376 OBP he posted were his best marks, respectively, since 2011. He was somewhat aided by a career-best .339 batting average on balls in play, but it wasn't that out of step with his career norms - in four other seasons, he's posted at least a .325 BABIP. The stolen bases will probably never come back — he's just 15-for-27 in stolen-base attempts the last three seasons — but he should definitely be near the top of your second-base pecking order come draft day.<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>It was a renaissance year for Dustin Pedroia, who recaptured the glory of his 2008-2011 heyday. He probably won't be quite as good in 2017, but he should still easily be a top-10 second baseman, if not better.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

He'll be an incredibly tough guy to rank in 2015, with his ranking hinging on the health and strength of his arms. Let him go if someone wants to draft him among the top six or so second basemen, there's too much risk there. He tried to come back after essentially a minimum stay but went just 1-for-23 before heading right back for the same injury -- a strained right hamstring -- for another month-plus. However, he did finish his season on the field and closed with a bang, posting a .308/.386/.500 in 88 plate appearances. While the hamstring tanked the season from a volume standpoint, his base skills held firm and his isolated slugging percentage was actually a three-year high at .150. At 32, Pedroia is in that phase of career that Chase Utley just finished where we must plan for time missed, but the time you do get will still yield strong stats. Pedroia probably won't continue to run as Utley did -- you could say Pedroia has already stopped as he's been just 8-for-16 the last two years -- but his average should remain high, so it works out as a trade-off. Additionally, there is a measure of depth at second base that makes securing a backup much easier. But in both 2013 and '14, he failed to crack double digits in home runs, and in 2014 his doubles were down, and he failed to record a triple for the first time in any full major league season. His power evaporated, and his walk rate slipped - the .337 on-base percentage he posted in 2014 was easily a full-season worst. He turned things around somewhat in 2015, but he got hurt and only logged 93 games, an unfathomable total for the gritty-to-the-point-of-reckless Pedroia. But in 2016, everything clicked back into place. The 120 wRC+ and .376 OBP he posted were his best marks, respectively, since 2011.
